<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Biological process of getting older</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about ageing specifically in humans. For the ageing of whole organisms including animals, see <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">Senescence</a>.</div>

<p><b>Ageing</b> (<a href="/wiki/British_English" title="British English">BE</a>) or <b>aging</b> (<a href="/wiki/American_English" title="American English">AE</a>) is the process of becoming <a href="/wiki/Old_age" title="Old age">older</a>. The term refers mainly to <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">humans</a>, many other <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, whereas for example, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perennial_plants" title="Perennial plants">perennial plants</a> and some simple animals are potentially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biologically_immortal" title="Biologically immortal">biologically immortal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> In broader senses, ageing can refer to single cells within an organism which have <a href="/wiki/Cellular_senescence" title="Cellular senescence">ceased dividing</a>, or to the <a href="/wiki/Population_ageing" title="Population ageing">population of a species</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>In humans, ageing represents the accumulation of changes in a human being over time<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen_2004_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen_2004-3">[3]</a></sup> and can encompass physical, psychological, and social changes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Reaction time, for example, may slow with age, while memories and general knowledge typically increase. Ageing increases the risk of human diseases such as <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aging-associated_diseases" title="Aging-associated diseases">many more</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Of the roughly 150,000 people who die each day across the globe, about two-thirds die from age-related causes.
</p><p>Current <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_aging" title="Theory of aging">ageing theories</a> are assigned to the damage concept, whereby the accumulation of damage (such as <a href="/wiki/DNA_oxidation" title="DNA oxidation">DNA oxidation</a>) may cause biological systems to fail, or to the programmed ageing concept, whereby the internal processes (epigenetic maintenance such as <a href="/wiki/DNA_methylation" title="DNA methylation">DNA methylation</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> inherently may cause ageing. Programmed ageing should not be confused with programmed cell death (<a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a>).
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">Obesity</a> has been proposed to accelerate ageing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> whereas dietary <a href="/wiki/Calorie_restriction" title="Calorie restriction">calorie restriction</a> in non-primate animals slows ageing while maintaining good health and body functions. In primates (including humans), such <a href="/wiki/Life_extension" title="Life extension">life-extending</a> effects remain uncertain.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ageing_versus_immortality">Ageing versus immortality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Ageing versus immortality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hydras_(8).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="2051" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hydras_%288%29.JPG/170px-Hydras_%288%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hydras_%288%29.JPG/255px-Hydras_%288%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Hydras_%288%29.JPG/340px-Hydras_%288%29.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hydras_(8).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Immortal <i><a href="/wiki/Hydra_(genus)" title="Hydra (genus)">Hydra</a></i>, a relative of the jellyfish</div></div></div>
<p>Human beings and members of other species, especially animals, age and die. Fungi, too, can age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mortimer_1959_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mortimer_1959-12">[12]</a></sup> In contrast, many species can be considered potentially <a href="/wiki/Biological_immortality" title="Biological immortality">immortal</a>: for example, <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> fission to produce daughter cells, <a href="/wiki/Strawberry" title="Strawberry">strawberry</a> plants grow runners to produce clones of themselves, and animals in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Hydra_(genus)" title="Hydra (genus)">Hydra</a></i> have a regenerative ability by which they avoid dying of old age.
</p><p>Early life forms on Earth, starting at least 3.7 billion years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nutman_2016_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nutman_2016-13">[13]</a></sup> were single-celled organisms (an organism that consists of a single cell, unlike a multicellular organism that consists of multiple cells). Such organisms (<a href="/wiki/Prokaryote" title="Prokaryote">Prokaryotes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">Protozoans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">algae</a>) multiply by <a href="/wiki/Fission_(biology)" title="Fission (biology)">fission</a> into daughter cells(Daughter cells are cells that are the result of a single dividing parent cell. Two daughter cells are the result from the mitotic process while four cells are the result from the meiotic process. For organisms that reproduce via sexual reproduction, daughter cells result from meiosis); thus do not age and are potentially immortal under favorable conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rose1991_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rose1991-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Partridge1993_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Partridge1993-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ageing and mortality of the individual organism became possible with the <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_sexual_reproduction" title="Evolution of sexual reproduction">evolution of sexual reproduction</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams1957_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams1957-16">[16]</a></sup> which occurred with the emergence of the fungal/animal kingdoms approximately a billion years ago, and the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Spermatophyte" title="Spermatophyte">seed-producing plants</a> 320 million years ago. The sexual organism could henceforth pass on some of its genetic material to produce new individuals and could itself <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_ageing#Disposable_soma_theory" title="Evolution of ageing">become disposable</a> with respect to the survival of its species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams1957_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams1957-16">[16]</a></sup> This classic biological idea has however been perturbed recently by the discovery that the bacterium <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E._coli" title="E. coli">E. coli</a></i> may split into distinguishable daughter cells, which opens the theoretical possibility of "age classes" among bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stewart2005_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stewart2005-17">[17]</a></sup>
</p><p>Even within humans and other mortal species, there are cells with the potential for immortality: cancer cells which have lost the ability to die when maintained in a cell culture such as the <a href="/wiki/HeLa" title="HeLa">HeLa</a> cell line,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PereiraSmith1992_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-PereiraSmith1992-18">[18]</a></sup> and specific <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cells</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Germ_cell" title="Germ cell">germ cells</a> (producing <a href="/wiki/Egg_cell" title="Egg cell">ova</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">spermatozoa</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Forster_2015_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Forster_2015-19">[19]</a></sup> In artificial <a href="/wiki/Cloning" title="Cloning">cloning</a>, adult cells can be rejuvenated to embryonic status and then used to grow a new tissue or animal without ageing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wakayama_2013_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wakayama_2013-20">[20]</a></sup> Normal human cells however die after about 50 cell divisions in laboratory culture (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hayflick_Limit" title="Hayflick Limit">Hayflick Limit</a>, discovered by <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Hayflick" title="Leonard Hayflick">Leonard Hayflick</a> in 1961).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PereiraSmith1992_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-PereiraSmith1992-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs">Signs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Signs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Old_age#Signs" title="Old age">Old age § Signs</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Senescence.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1329" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Senescence.JPG/170px-Senescence.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Senescence.JPG/255px-Senescence.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Senescence.JPG/340px-Senescence.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Senescence.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Enlarged ears and noses of old humans are sometimes blamed on continual cartilage growth, but the cause is more probably gravity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian2013_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian2013-21">[21]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Age_dynamics_of_the_body_mass.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="397" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Age_dynamics_of_the_body_mass.svg/220px-Age_dynamics_of_the_body_mass.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Age_dynamics_of_the_body_mass.svg/330px-Age_dynamics_of_the_body_mass.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Age_dynamics_of_the_body_mass.svg/440px-Age_dynamics_of_the_body_mass.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Age_dynamics_of_the_body_mass.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Age dynamics of the body mass (1, 2) and mass normalized to height (3, 4) of men (1, 3) and women (2, 4)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Age_dynamics_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Age_dynamics-22">[22]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alzheimer%27s_disease_brain_comparison.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="3984" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Alzheimer%27s_disease_brain_comparison.jpg/220px-Alzheimer%27s_disease_brain_comparison.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Alzheimer%27s_disease_brain_comparison.jpg/330px-Alzheimer%27s_disease_brain_comparison.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Alzheimer%27s_disease_brain_comparison.jpg/440px-Alzheimer%27s_disease_brain_comparison.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alzheimer%27s_disease_brain_comparison.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Comparison of a normal aged brain (left) and a brain affected by <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a></div></div></div>
<p>A number of characteristic ageing symptoms are experienced by a majority or by a significant proportion of humans during their lifetimes.
</p>
<ul><li>Teenagers lose the young child's ability to <a href="/wiki/Presbycusis" title="Presbycusis">hear</a> high-frequency sounds above 20 kHz.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HiFreqAudiometry2014_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-HiFreqAudiometry2014-23">[23]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wrinkle" title="Wrinkle">Wrinkles</a> develop mainly due to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photoageing" title="Photoageing">photoageing</a>, particularly affecting sun-exposed areas (face).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thurstan_2012_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thurstan_2012-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li>After <a href="/wiki/Age_and_female_fertility" title="Age and female fertility">peaking from the late teens to the late 20s, female fertility</a> declines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></li>
<li>After age 30 the mass of human body is decreased until 70 years and then shows damping oscillations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Age_dynamics_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Age_dynamics-22">[22]</a></sup></li>
<li>People over 35 years of age are at increasing risk for losing strength in the <a href="/wiki/Ciliary_muscle" title="Ciliary muscle">ciliary muscle</a> of the eyes which leads to difficulty focusing on close objects, or <a href="/wiki/Presbyopia" title="Presbyopia">presbyopia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weale_2003_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weale_2003-27">[27]</a></sup> Most people experience <a href="/wiki/Presbyopia" title="Presbyopia">presbyopia</a> by age 45–50.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truscott_2009_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-Truscott_2009-28">[28]</a></sup> The cause is lens hardening by decreasing levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha-crystallin" title="Alpha-crystallin">alpha-crystallin</a>, a process which may be sped up by higher temperatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Truscott_2009_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-Truscott_2009-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li>
<li>Around age 50, <a href="/wiki/Human_hair_color#Aging_or_achromotrichia" title="Human hair color">hair turns grey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pattern_hair_loss" title="Pattern hair loss">Pattern hair loss</a> by the age of 50 affects about 30–50% of males<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> and a quarter of females.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Var2015_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Var2015-32">[32]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">Menopause</a> typically occurs between 44 and 58 years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li>
<li>In the 60–64 age cohort, the incidence of <a href="/wiki/Osteoarthritis" title="Osteoarthritis">osteoarthritis</a> rises to 53%. Only 20% however report disabling osteoarthritis at this age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas2014_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas2014-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>Almost half of people older than 75 have <a href="/wiki/Hearing_loss" title="Hearing loss">hearing loss</a> (presbycusis) inhibiting spoken communication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Many vertebrates such as fish, birds and amphibians do not develop presbycusis in old age as they are able to regenerate their <a href="/wiki/Cochlea" title="Cochlea">cochlear</a> sensory cells, whereas mammals including humans have genetically lost this ability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rubel2013_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rubel2013-36">[36]</a></sup></li>
<li>By age 80, more than half of all Americans either have a <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataract</a> or have had <a href="/wiki/Cataract_surgery" title="Cataract surgery">cataract surgery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2009_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-NIH2009-37">[37]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frailty_syndrome" title="Frailty syndrome">Frailty</a>, a syndrome of decreased strength, physical activity, physical performance and energy, affects 25% of those over 85.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fried_2001_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fried_2001-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Muscles have a reduced capacity of responding to exercise or injury and loss of muscle mass and strength (<a href="/wiki/Sarcopenia" title="Sarcopenia">sarcopenia</a>) is common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Maximum oxygen utilization and maximum heart rate decline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> Hand strength and mobility decrease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">Atherosclerosis</a> is classified as an ageing disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_2012_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang_2012-43">[43]</a></sup> It leads to cardiovascular disease (for example <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">stroke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attack</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_2016_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wang_2016-44">[44]</a></sup> which globally is the most common cause of death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Vessel ageing causes vascular remodeling and loss of arterial elasticity and as a result causes the stiffness of the vasculature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wang_2012_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wang_2012-43">[43]</a></sup></li>
<li>Recent evidence suggests that age-related risk of death plateaus after age 105.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The maximum human lifespan is suggested to be <a href="/wiki/Old_age" title="Old age">115 years</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20161005_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20161005-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NAT-20151005_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-NAT-20151005-48">[48]</a></sup> The oldest reliably recorded human was <a href="/wiki/Jeanne_Calment" title="Jeanne Calment">Jeanne Calment</a> who died in 1997 at 122.</li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dementia" title="Dementia">Dementia</a> becomes more common with age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Larson2013_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Larson2013-49">[49]</a></sup> About 3% of people between the ages of 65 and 74, 19% between 75 and 84, and nearly half of those over 85 years of age have dementia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The spectrum ranges from <a href="/wiki/Mild_cognitive_impairment" title="Mild cognitive impairment">mild cognitive impairment</a> to the neurodegenerative diseases of <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cerebrovascular_disease" title="Cerebrovascular disease">cerebrovascular disease</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">Parkinson's disease</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lou_Gehrig%27s_disease" title="Lou Gehrig's disease">Lou Gehrig's disease</a>. Furthermore, many types of <a href="/wiki/Memory_and_aging" title="Memory and aging">memory decline with ageing</a>, but not <a href="/wiki/Semantic_memory" title="Semantic memory">semantic memory</a> or general knowledge such as vocabulary definitions, which typically increases or remains steady until late adulthood<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schaie2005_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schaie2005-51">[51]</a></sup> (see <a href="/wiki/Aging_brain" title="Aging brain">Ageing brain</a>). <a href="/wiki/Intelligence" title="Intelligence">Intelligence</a> declines with age, though the rate varies depending on the <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_multiple_intelligences" title="Theory of multiple intelligences">type</a> and may in fact remain steady throughout most of the lifespan, dropping suddenly only as people near the end of their lives. Individual variations in rate of cognitive decline may therefore be explained in terms of people having different lengths of life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuart_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stuart-52">[52]</a></sup> There are changes to the brain: after 20 years of age there is a 10% reduction each decade in the total length of the brain's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myelinated" title="Myelinated">myelinated</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axons" title="Axons">axons</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marner_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marner-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>Age can result in <a href="/wiki/Visual_impairment" title="Visual impairment">visual impairment</a>, whereby <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-verbal_communication" title="Non-verbal communication">non-verbal communication</a> is reduced,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> which can lead to isolation and possible depression. Older adults, however, may not experience depression as much as younger adults, and were paradoxically found to have improved mood despite declining physical health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Macular_degeneration" title="Macular degeneration">Macular degeneration</a> causes vision loss and increases with age, affecting nearly 12% of those above the age of 80.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meh2015_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meh2015-57">[57]</a></sup> This degeneration is caused by systemic changes in the circulation of waste products and by growth of abnormal vessels around the retina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nussbaum,_J._F._1989_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nussbaum,_J._F._1989-58">[58]</a></sup> Other visual diseases that often appear with age would be cataracts and glaucoma. A cataract occurs when the lens of the eye becomes cloudy making vision blurry and eventually causing blindness if untreated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> They develop over time and are seen most often with those that are older. Cataracts can be treated through surgery. Glaucoma is another common visual disease that appears in older adults. Glaucoma is caused by damage to the optic nerve causing vision loss.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nei.nih.gov_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-nei.nih.gov-60">[60]</a></sup> Glaucoma usually develops over time but there are variations to glaucoma, and some have sudden onset. There are a few procedures for glaucoma but there is no cure or fix for the damage once it has happened. Prevention is the best measure in the case of glaucoma.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nei.nih.gov_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-nei.nih.gov-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>A distinction can be made between "proximal ageing" (age-based effects that come about because of factors in the recent past) and "distal ageing" (age-based differences that can be traced to a cause in a person's early life, such as childhood <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poliomyelitis" title="Poliomyelitis">poliomyelitis</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuart_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stuart-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ageing is among the greatest known risk factors for most human <a href="/wiki/Aging-associated_diseases" title="Aging-associated diseases">diseases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aleksey_V_2018_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aleksey_V_2018-61">[61]</a></sup> Of the roughly 150,000 people who die each day across the globe, about two-thirds—100,000 per day—die from <a href="/wiki/Aging-associated_diseases" title="Aging-associated diseases">age-related causes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Grey_2007_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-de_Grey_2007-62">[62]</a></sup> In industrialized nations, the proportion is higher, reaching 90%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-de_Grey_2007_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-de_Grey_2007-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopez_2006_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lopez_2006-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_basis">Biological basis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Biological basis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">Senescence</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Old_woman_with_young_baby_boy.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Old_woman_with_young_baby_boy.JPG/170px-Old_woman_with_young_baby_boy.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Old_woman_with_young_baby_boy.JPG/255px-Old_woman_with_young_baby_boy.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Old_woman_with_young_baby_boy.JPG/340px-Old_woman_with_young_baby_boy.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Old_woman_with_young_baby_boy.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>95-year-old woman holding a five-month-old boy</div></div></div>
<p>At present, researchers are only just beginning to understand the biological basis of ageing even in relatively simple and short-lived organisms such as yeast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janssens_2015_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janssens_2015-65">[65]</a></sup> Less still is known of mammalian ageing, in part due to the much longer lives of even small mammals such as the mouse (around 3 years). A <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organism</a> for the study of ageing is the <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Caenorhabditis_elegans" title="Caenorhabditis elegans">C. elegans</a></i>. Thanks to its short lifespan of 2–3 weeks, our ability to easily perform genetic manipulations or to suppress gene activity with <a href="/wiki/RNA_interference" title="RNA interference">RNA interference</a>, or other factors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RothmanSingson2012_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-RothmanSingson2012-66">[66]</a></sup> Most known mutations and RNA interference targets that extend lifespan were first discovered in <i>C. elegans</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reis2009_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reis2009-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>The factors proposed to influence biological ageing<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="This claim needs references to better sources. (May 2021)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> fall into two main categories, <i>programmed</i> and <i>damage-related</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jin_2010_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jin_2010-69">[69]</a></sup> Programmed factors follow a biological timetable, perhaps one that might be a continuation of the one that regulates childhood growth and development. This regulation would depend on changes in gene expression that affect the systems responsible for maintenance, repair and defense responses. Damage-related factors include internal and environmental assaults to living organisms that induce cumulative damage at various levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jin_2010_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jin_2010-69">[69]</a></sup> A third, novel, concept is that ageing is mediated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Virtuous_circle_and_vicious_circle" title="Virtuous circle and vicious circle">vicious cycles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aleksey_V_2018_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aleksey_V_2018-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Molecular_and_cellular_hallmarks_of_ageing">Molecular and cellular hallmarks of ageing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Molecular and cellular hallmarks of ageing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hallmarks_of_aging" title="Hallmarks of aging">Hallmarks of aging</a></div>
<p>In a detailed review, Lopez-Otin and colleagues (2013), who discuss ageing through the lens of the damage theory, propose nine metabolic "hallmarks" of ageing in various organisms but especially mammals:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lopez-Otin2013_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lopez-Otin2013-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Genome_instability" title="Genome instability">genomic instability</a> (mutations accumulated in nuclear DNA, in mtDNA, and in the nuclear lamina)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Telomere" title="Telomere">telomere</a> attrition (the authors note that artificial telomerase confers non-cancerous immortality to otherwise mortal cells)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Epigenetics" title="Epigenetics">epigenetic</a> alterations (including DNA methylation patterns, post-translational modification of histones, and chromatin remodelling)</li>
<li>loss of <a href="/wiki/Proteostasis" title="Proteostasis">proteostasis</a> (protein folding and proteolysis)</li>
<li>deregulated nutrient sensing (relating to the <a href="/wiki/Insulin-like_growth_factor_1#Aging" title="Insulin-like growth factor 1">Growth hormone/Insulin-like growth factor 1 signalling pathway</a>, which is the most conserved ageing-controlling pathway in evolution and among its targets are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/FOXO" title="FOXO">FOXO</a>3/<a href="/wiki/Sirtuin" title="Sirtuin">Sirtuin</a> transcription factors and the <a href="/wiki/MTOR" title="MTOR">mTOR</a> complexes, probably responsive to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caloric_restriction" title="Caloric restriction">caloric restriction</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_disease" title="Mitochondrial disease">mitochondrial dysfunction</a> (the authors point out however that a causal link between ageing and increased mitochondrial production of reactive oxygen species is no longer supported by recent research)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cellular_senescence" title="Cellular senescence">cellular senescence</a> (accumulation of no longer dividing cells in certain tissues, a process induced especially by <a href="/wiki/P16" title="P16">p16INK4a</a>/Rb and p19ARF/<a href="/wiki/P53" title="P53">p53</a> to stop cancerous cells from proliferating)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Stem_cell" title="Stem cell">stem cell</a> exhaustion (in the authors' view caused by damage factors such as those listed above)</li>
<li>altered intercellular communication (encompassing especially inflammation but possibly also other intercellular interactions</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflammageing" title="Inflammageing">inflammageing</a>, a chronic inflammatory phenotype in the elderly in the absence of viral infection, due to over-activation and a decrease in the precision of the innate immune system.</li>
<li>dysbiosis of gut <a href="/wiki/Microbiome" title="Microbiome">microbiome</a> (e.g., loss of microbial diversity, expansion of enteropathogens, and altered vitamin B12 biosynthesis) is correlated with biological age rather than chronological age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ratiner2022_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ratiner2022-71">[71]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metabolic_pathways_involved_in_ageing">Metabolic pathways involved in ageing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Metabolic pathways involved in ageing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are three main metabolic pathways which can influence the rate of ageing, discussed below:
</p>
<ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/FOXO3" title="FOXO3">FOXO3</a>/<a href="/wiki/Sirtuin" title="Sirtuin">Sirtuin</a> pathway, probably responsive to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caloric_restriction" title="Caloric restriction">caloric restriction</a></li>
<li>the <a href="/wiki/Insulin-like_growth_factor_1#Aging" title="Insulin-like growth factor 1">Growth hormone/Insulin-like growth factor 1 signalling pathway</a></li>
<li>the activity levels of the <a href="/wiki/Electron_transport_chain" title="Electron transport chain">electron transport chain</a> in mitochondria<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> and (in plants) in chloroplasts.</li></ul>
<p>It is likely that most of these pathways affect ageing separately, because targeting them simultaneously leads to additive increases in lifespan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Programmed_factors">Programmed factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Programmed factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The rate of ageing varies substantially across different species, and this, to a large extent, is genetically based. For example, numerous <a href="/wiki/Perennial_plant" title="Perennial plant">perennial plants</a> ranging from strawberries and potatoes to <a href="/wiki/Willow#Cultivation" title="Willow">willow trees</a> typically produce clones of themselves by <a href="/wiki/Vegetative_reproduction" title="Vegetative reproduction">vegetative reproduction</a> and are thus potentially immortal, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annual_plants" title="Annual plants">annual plants</a> such as wheat and watermelons die each year and reproduce by sexual reproduction. In 2008 it was discovered that inactivation of only two genes in the annual plant <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> leads to its conversion into a potentially immortal perennial plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> The oldest animals known so far are 15,000-year-old Antarctic <a href="/wiki/Sponge" title="Sponge">sponges</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC2017-OldestLivingThing_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2017-OldestLivingThing-75">[75]</a></sup> which can reproduce both sexually and clonally.
</p><p>Clonal immortality apart, there are certain species whose individual lifespans stand out among Earth's life-forms, including the <a href="/wiki/Bristlecone_pine" title="Bristlecone pine">bristlecone pine</a> at 5062 years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oldest_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-oldest-76">[76]</a></sup> or 5067 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC2017-OldestLivingThing_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2017-OldestLivingThing-75">[75]</a></sup> invertebrates like the <a href="/wiki/Hard_clam" title="Hard clam">hard clam</a> (known as <i>quahog</i> in New England) at 508 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sosnowska2014_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sosnowska2014-77">[77]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_shark" title="Greenland shark">Greenland shark</a> at 400 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nielsen2016_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nielsen2016-78">[78]</a></sup> various deep-sea <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tube_worms" title="Tube worms">tube worms</a> at over 300 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Durkin2017_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Durkin2017-79">[79]</a></sup> fish like the <a href="/wiki/Sturgeon" title="Sturgeon">sturgeon</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sebastes" title="Sebastes">rockfish</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sea_anemone" title="Sea anemone">sea anemone</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Lobster" title="Lobster">lobster</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Such organisms are sometimes said to exhibit <a href="/wiki/Negligible_senescence" title="Negligible senescence">negligible senescence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The genetic aspect has also been demonstrated in studies of human <a href="/wiki/Research_into_centenarians" title="Research into centenarians">centenarians</a>.
</p><p>In laboratory settings, researchers have demonstrated that selected alterations in specific <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> can extend lifespan quite substantially in <a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae#In_biological_research" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">yeast</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nematodes" title="Nematodes">roundworms</a>, less so in <a href="/wiki/Drosophila_melanogaster" title="Drosophila melanogaster">fruit flies</a> and less again in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mice" title="Mice">mice</a>.  Some of the targeted genes have <a href="/wiki/Homology_(biology)" title="Homology (biology)">homologues</a> across species and in some cases have been associated with human longevity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartke2011_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartke2011-84">[84]</a></sup> Studies by Becca Levy, an associate professor of epidemiology and psychology at the <a href="/wiki/Yale_School_of_Public_Health" title="Yale School of Public Health">Yale School of Public Health</a>, have found that positive beliefs about ageing may also increase life span.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Epigenetic_clock" title="Epigenetic clock">DNA methylation</a>: The strong effect of age on DNA methylation levels has been known since the late 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berdyshev1967_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berdyshev1967-86">[86]</a></sup> Geneticist <a href="/wiki/Steve_Horvath" title="Steve Horvath">Steve Horvath</a> hypothesised that DNA methylation age measures the cumulative effect of an epigenetic maintenance system but details are unknown. DNA methylation age of blood predicts all-cause mortality in later life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marioni2015mortality_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marioni2015mortality-87">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Christiansen2015_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Christiansen2015-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horvath2015centenarian_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Horvath2015centenarian-89">[89]</a></sup> The epigenetic clock theory of ageing recognizes ageing as a by-product or unintended consequence of developmental and maintenance processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Furthermore, prematurely aged mice can be rejuvenated and their lives extended by 30% by partially "resetting" the methylation pattern in their cells (a full reset leads to undesirable immortal cancer cells). This resetting into a juvenile state was experimentally achieved in 2016 by activating the four <a href="/wiki/Shinya_Yamanaka" title="Shinya Yamanaka">Yamanaka</a> DNA transcription factors – <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sox2" title="Sox2">Sox2</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oct4" title="Oct4">Oct4</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Klf4" title="Klf4">Klf4</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C-Myc" title="C-Myc">c-Myc</a> (which have previously been routinely used for producing young animals from cloned adult skin cells).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ocampo2016_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ocampo2016-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScienceNewsMethylation2016_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScienceNewsMethylation2016-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>Senescent cells: most of the cells with DNA damages that can't be fixed do apoptosis but some cells don't. Those cells are related to many diseases such as kidney failure and diabetes. In 2016, in a study, the removal of those cells in mice has extended their lifespans by 20% to 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Another study shows that this issue is related to the p16<sup>INK4a</sup> and <a href="/wiki/Beta-galactosidase" title="Beta-galactosidase">β-galactosidase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Significant results have been obtained by some companies to extend mouse lifespan focusing on senescent cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li>A variation in the gene <a href="/wiki/FOXO3" title="FOXO3">FOXO3</a>A has a positive effect on the life expectancy of humans, and is found much more often in people living to 100 and beyond – moreover, this appears to be true worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> FOXO3A acts on the <a href="/wiki/Sirtuin" title="Sirtuin">sirtuin</a> family of genes which also have a significant effect on lifespan in <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeast</a> and in <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>. Sirtuin in turn inhibits <a href="/wiki/MTOR" title="MTOR">mTOR</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ghosh2010_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ghosh2010-99">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caloric_restriction" title="Caloric restriction">Caloric restriction</a> leads to longer lifespans in various species, an effect that is unclear,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor2011_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor2011-73">[73]</a></sup> but probably mediated by the nutrient-sensing function of the mTOR pathway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fontana2010_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fontana2010-100">[100]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/MTOR" title="MTOR">mTOR</a>, a protein that inhibits <a href="/wiki/Autophagy" title="Autophagy">autophagy</a>, has been linked to ageing through the <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> signalling pathway. mTOR functions through nutrient and growth cues leading scientists to believe that dietary restriction and mTOR are related in terms of longevity.  When organisms restrict their diet, mTOR activity is reduced, which allows an increased level of autophagy. This recycles old or damaged cell parts, which increases longevity and decreases the chances of being obese. This is thought to prevent spikes of <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> concentration in the blood, leading to reduced insulin signalling. This has been linked to less mTOR activation as well.  Therefore, longevity has been connected to caloric restriction and insulin sensitivity inhibiting mTOR, which in turns allows autophagy to occur more frequently.  It may be that mTOR inhibition and autophagy reduce the effects of <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> on the body, which damage DNA and other organic material, so longevity would be increased.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Johnson_et_al_2013_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Johnson_et_al_2013-101">[101]</a></sup> In support of this contention are observations that several purported anti-ageing remedies such as rapamycin, metformin, berberine, 2-deoxyglucose, vitamin D3, aspirin and resveratrol were shown to suppress mTOR signaling and concurrently to reduce constitutive level of oxidative DNA damage induced by endogenous oxidants as well as to enhance the rate of autophagy<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></li>
<li>A decreased <a href="/wiki/Insulin-like_growth_factor_1#Aging" title="Insulin-like growth factor 1">Growth hormone/Insulin-like Growth Factor 1 signalling pathway</a> has been associated with increased life span in various organisms including fruit flies, nematodes and mice. The precise mechanism by which decreased GH/IGF-1 signalling increases longevity is unknown, but various mouse strains with decreased GH and/or IGF-1 induced signalling share a similar phenotype which includes increased insulin sensitivity, enhanced stress resistance and protection from carcinogenesis. The studied mouse strains with decreased GH signalling showed between 20% and 68% increased longevity, and mouse strains with decreased IGF-1 induced signalling revealed a 19 to 33% increase in life span when compared to control mice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junnila2013_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-Junnila2013-103">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li>Over-expression of the <a href="/wiki/Ras2" title="Ras2">Ras2</a> gene increases lifespan in <a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae#In_biological_research" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">yeast</a> by 30%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sun_1994_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sun_1994-104">[104]</a></sup> A yeast mutant lacking the genes SCH9 and RAS1 has recently been shown to have a tenfold increase in lifespan under conditions of calorie restriction and is the largest increase achieved in any organism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Telomere" title="Telomere">Telomeres</a>: In humans and other animals, cellular <a href="/wiki/Senescence" title="Senescence">senescence</a> has been attributed to the shortening of telomeres at each <a href="/wiki/Cell_cycle" title="Cell cycle">cell division</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> when telomeres become too short, the cells senesce and die or cease multiplying.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mikhelson_2012_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mikhelson_2012-108">[108]</a></sup> The length of telomeres is therefore the "molecular clock", predicted by Hayflick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_book_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein_book-110">[110]</a></sup> However, telomere length in wild mouse strains is unrelated to lifespan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> and mice lacking the enzyme telomerase do not have a dramatically reduced lifespan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Laboratory mice's telomeres are many times longer than human ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Another caveat is that a study following nearly 1000 humans for ten years showed that while some humans do shorten their telomeres over time, a third of the participants did not.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></li></ul>
<ul><li><ul><li>The theory would explain why the <a href="/wiki/Dominance_(genetics)" title="Dominance (genetics)">autosomal dominant</a> disease, <a href="/wiki/Huntington%27s_disease" title="Huntington's disease">Huntington's disease</a>, can persist even though it is inexorably lethal. Also, it has been suggested that some of the genetic variants that increase fertility in the young increase cancer risk in the old.  Such variants occur in genes p53<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kang_2009_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kang_2009-115">[115]</a></sup> and BRCA1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smith_2012_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smith_2012-116">[116]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Reproductive-cell_cycle_theory" title="Reproductive-cell cycle theory">reproductive-cell cycle theory</a> argues that ageing is regulated specifically by reproductive hormones that act in an antagonistic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pleiotropic" title="Pleiotropic">pleiotropic</a> manner via cell cycle signalling, promoting growth and development early in life to achieve reproduction, but becoming dysregulated later in life, driving senescence (dyosis) in a futile attempt to maintain reproductive ability.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen_2004_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen_2004-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid20851172_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20851172-117">[117]</a></sup> The endocrine dyscrasia that follows the loss of follicles with menopause, and the loss of Leydig and Sertoli cells during andropause, drive aberrant cell cycle signalling that leads to cell death and dysfunction, tissue dysfunction (disease) and ultimately death. Moreover, the hormones that regulate reproduction also regulate cellular metabolism, explaining the increases in fat deposition during pregnancy through to the deposition of centralised adiposity with the dysregulation of the HPG axis following menopause and during andropause (Atwood and Bowen, 2004). This theory, which introduced a new definition of ageing, has facilitated the conceptualisation of why and how ageing occurs at the evolutionary, physiological and molecular levels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowen_2004_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bowen_2004-3">[3]</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li>Autoimmunity: The idea that ageing results from an increase in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autoantibodies" title="Autoantibodies">autoantibodies</a> that attack the body's tissues. A number of diseases associated with ageing, such as <a href="/wiki/Atrophic_gastritis" title="Atrophic gastritis">atrophic gastritis</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hashimoto%27s_thyroiditis" title="Hashimoto's thyroiditis">Hashimoto's thyroiditis</a>, are probably autoimmune in this way.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> However, while inflammation is very much evident in old mammals, even completely <a href="/wiki/Severe_combined_immunodeficiency#SCID_in_animals" title="Severe combined immunodeficiency">immunodeficient</a> mice raised in <a href="/wiki/Specific-pathogen-free" title="Specific-pathogen-free">pathogen-free</a> laboratory conditions still experience senescence.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li></ul>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Habibaadansalat.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="4278" decoding="async" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Habibaadansalat.jpg/220px-Habibaadansalat.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Habibaadansalat.jpg/330px-Habibaadansalat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Habibaadansalat.jpg/440px-Habibaadansalat.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Habibaadansalat.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An elderly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somali_people" title="Somali people">Somali</a> woman</div></div></div>
<ul><li>The cellular balance between energy generation and consumption (energy homeostasis) requires tight regulation during ageing. In 2011, it was demonstrated that acetylation levels of <a href="/wiki/AMP-activated_protein_kinase" title="AMP-activated protein kinase">AMP-activated protein kinase</a> change with age in yeast and that preventing this change slows yeast ageing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mair2011_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mair2011-119">[119]</a></sup></li>
<li>Skin ageing is caused in part by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TGF-%CE%B2" title="TGF-β">TGF-β</a>, which reduces the subcutaneous fat that gives skin a pleasant appearance and texture.   <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TGF-%CE%B2" title="TGF-β">TGF-β</a> does this by blocking the conversion of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dermal_fibroblasts" title="Dermal fibroblasts">dermal fibroblasts</a> into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fat_cells" title="Fat cells">fat cells</a>; with fewer fat cells underneath to provide support, the skin becomes saggy and wrinkled.  Subcutaneous fat also produces <a href="/wiki/Cathelicidin" title="Cathelicidin">cathelicidin</a>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Peptide" title="Peptide">peptide</a> that fights bacterial infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolution_of_ageing">Evolution of ageing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Evolution of ageing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Evolution_of_ageing" title="Evolution of ageing">Evolution of ageing</a></div>
<p>Life span, like other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenotypes" title="Phenotypes">phenotypes</a>, is <a href="/wiki/Natural_selection" title="Natural selection">selected</a> for in evolution. Traits that benefit early survival and reproduction will be selected for even if they contribute to an earlier death. Such a genetic effect is called the <a href="/wiki/Antagonistic_pleiotropy_hypothesis" title="Antagonistic pleiotropy hypothesis">antagonistic pleiotropy</a> effect when referring to a gene (pleiotropy signifying the gene has a double function – enabling reproduction at a young age but costing the organism life expectancy in old age) and is called the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evolution_of_aging" title="Evolution of aging">disposable soma</a> effect when referring to an entire genetic programme (the organism diverting limited resources from maintenance to reproduction).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams1957_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Williams1957-16">[16]</a></sup> The biological mechanisms which regulate lifespan probably evolved with the first multicellular organisms more than a billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reis2009_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Reis2009-67">[67]</a></sup> However, even single-celled organisms such as yeast have been used as models in ageing, hence ageing has its biological roots much earlier than multi-cellularity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Damage-related_factors">Damage-related factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Damage-related factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/DNA_damage_theory_of_aging" title="DNA damage theory of aging">DNA damage theory of ageing</a>: DNA damage is thought to be the common basis of both cancer and ageing, and it has been argued that intrinsic causes of <a href="/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring)" title="DNA damage (naturally occurring)">DNA damage</a> are the most important drivers of ageing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Genetic damage (aberrant structural alterations of the DNA), <a href="/wiki/Mutation" title="Mutation">mutations</a> (changes in the DNA sequence), and epimutations (<a href="/wiki/Cancer_epigenetics#DNA_methylation" title="Cancer epigenetics">methylation of gene promoter regions</a> or alterations of the <a href="/wiki/Cancer_epigenetics#Histone_modification" title="Cancer epigenetics">DNA scaffolding</a> which <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_gene_expression" title="Regulation of gene expression">regulate gene expression</a>), can cause abnormal gene expression. DNA damage causes the cells to stop dividing or induces <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a>, often <a href="/wiki/Stem_cell_theory_of_aging" title="Stem cell theory of aging">affecting stem cell pools</a> and hence hindering regeneration. However, lifelong studies of mice suggest that most mutations happen during embryonic and childhood development, when cells divide often, as each cell division is a chance for errors in DNA replication.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genetic_instability" title="Genetic instability">Genetic instability</a>: Dogs annually lose approximately 3.3% of the DNA in their heart muscle cells while humans lose approximately 0.6% of their heart muscle DNA each year.  These numbers are close to the ratio of the maximum longevities of the two species (120 years vs. 20 years, a 6/1 ratio).  The comparative percentage is also similar between the dog and human for yearly DNA loss in the brain and lymphocytes.  As stated by lead author, <a href="/wiki/Bernard_L._Strehler" title="Bernard L. Strehler">Bernard L. Strehler</a>, "... genetic damage (particularly gene loss) is almost certainly (or probably the) central cause of ageing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></li>
<li>Accumulation of waste:
<ul><li>A buildup of waste products in cells presumably interferes with metabolism. For example, a waste product called <a href="/wiki/Lipofuscin" title="Lipofuscin">lipofuscin</a> is formed by a complex reaction in cells that binds fat to proteins. This waste accumulates in the cells as small granules, which increase in size as a person ages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup></li>
<li>The hallmark of ageing yeast cells appears to be overproduction of certain proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janssens_2015_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-Janssens_2015-65">[65]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Autophagy" title="Autophagy">Autophagy</a> induction can enhance clearance of toxic intracellular waste associated with neurodegenerative diseases and has been comprehensively demonstrated to improve lifespan in yeast, worms, flies, rodents and primates. The situation, however, has been complicated by the identification that autophagy up-regulation can also occur during ageing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carroll_2013_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carroll_2013-129">[129]</a></sup> Autophagy is enhanced in obese mice by caloric restriction, exercise, and a low fat diet (but in these mice is evidently not related with the activation of <a href="/wiki/AMP-activated_protein_kinase" title="AMP-activated protein kinase">AMP-activated protein kinase</a>, see above).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cui_2013_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cui_2013-130">[130]</a></sup></li></ul></li>
<li>Wear-and-tear theory: The very general idea that changes associated with ageing are the result of chance damage that accumulates over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jin_2010_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Jin_2010-69">[69]</a></sup></li>
<li>Accumulation of errors: The idea that ageing results from chance events that escape proof reading mechanisms, which gradually damages the genetic code.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Heterochromatin" title="Heterochromatin">Heterochromatin</a> loss, model of ageing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transposable_elements" title="Transposable elements">Transposable elements</a> in genome disintegration as the primary role in the mechanism of ageing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></li>
<li>Cross-linkage: The idea that ageing results from accumulation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cross-linked" title="Cross-linked">cross-linked</a> compounds that interfere with normal cell function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_book_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein_book-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup></li>
<li>Studies of mtDNA mutator mice have shown that increased levels of somatic mtDNA mutations directly can cause a variety of ageing phenotypes. The authors propose that mtDNA mutations lead to respiratory-chain-deficient cells and thence to apoptosis and cell loss. They cast doubt experimentally however on the common assumption that mitochondrial mutations and dysfunction lead to increased generation of reactive oxygen species (ROS).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free-radical_theory" title="Free-radical theory">Free-radical theory</a>: Damage by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">free radicals</a>, or more generally <a href="/wiki/Reactive_oxygen_species" title="Reactive oxygen species">reactive oxygen species</a> or <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_stress" title="Oxidative stress">oxidative stress</a>, create damage that may give rise to the symptoms we recognise as ageing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein_book_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein_book-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Michael_Ristow" title="Michael Ristow">Michael Ristow</a>'s group has provided evidence that the effect of calorie restriction may be due to increased formation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radicals" title="Free radicals">free radicals</a> within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondria</a>, causing a secondary induction of increased <a href="/wiki/Antioxidant" title="Antioxidant">antioxidant</a> defence capacity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_theory_of_ageing" title="Mitochondrial theory of ageing">Mitochondrial theory of ageing</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radicals" title="Free radicals">free radicals</a> produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mitochondria" title="Mitochondria">mitochondrial</a> activity damage cellular components, leading to ageing.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/8-Oxo-2%27-deoxyguanosine" title="8-Oxo-2'-deoxyguanosine">DNA oxidation</a> and caloric restriction: Caloric restriction reduces <a href="/wiki/8-Oxo-2%27-deoxyguanosine" title="8-Oxo-2'-deoxyguanosine">8-OH-dG</a> DNA damage in organs of ageing rats and mice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid11517304_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid11517304-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15763395_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15763395-142">[142]</a></sup> Thus, reduction of oxidative DNA damage is associated with a slower rate of ageing and increased lifespan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>  In a 2021 review article, Vijg stated that "Based on an abundance of evidence, <a href="/wiki/DNA_damage_(naturally_occurring)" title="DNA damage (naturally occurring)">DNA damage</a> is now considered as the single most important driver of the degenerative processes that collectively cause aging."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention_and_delay">Prevention and delay</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Prevention and delay">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Life_extension" title="Life extension">Life extension</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifestyle">Lifestyle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Lifestyle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caloric_restriction" title="Caloric restriction">Caloric restriction</a> substantially affects lifespan in many animals, including the ability to delay or prevent many age-related diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guarente2005_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guarente2005-145">[145]</a></sup> Typically, this involves caloric intake of 60–70% of what an <i><a href="/wiki/Ad_libitum#Biology" title="Ad libitum">ad libitum</a></i> animal would consume, while still maintaining proper nutrient intake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guarente2005_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guarente2005-145">[145]</a></sup> In rodents, this has been shown to increase lifespan by up to 50%;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agarwal2011_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agarwal2011-146">[146]</a></sup> similar effects occur for yeast and <i>Drosophila</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guarente2005_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guarente2005-145">[145]</a></sup> No lifespan data exist for humans on a calorie-restricted diet,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junnila2013_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-Junnila2013-103">[103]</a></sup> but several reports support protection from age-related diseases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Two major ongoing studies on <a href="/wiki/Rhesus_macaque" title="Rhesus macaque">rhesus monkeys</a> initially revealed disparate results; while one study, by the University of Wisconsin, showed that caloric restriction does extend lifespan,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colman2009_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colman2009-149">[149]</a></sup> the second study, by the National Institute on Aging (NIA), found no effects of caloric restriction on longevity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> Both studies nevertheless showed improvement in a number of health parameters. Notwithstanding the similarly low calorie intake, the diet composition differed between the two studies (notably a high <a href="/wiki/Sucrose" title="Sucrose">sucrose</a> content in the Wisconsin study), and the monkeys have different origins (India, China), initially suggesting that genetics and dietary composition, not merely a decrease in calories, are factors in longevity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Junnila2013_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-Junnila2013-103">[103]</a></sup> However, in a comparative analysis in 2014, the Wisconsin researchers found that the allegedly non-starved NIA control monkeys in fact are moderately underweight when compared with other monkey populations, and argued this was due to the NIA's apportioned feeding protocol in contrast to Wisconsin's truly unrestricted <i>ad libitum</i> feeding protocol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Colman2014_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-Colman2014-151">[151]</a></sup> They conclude that moderate calorie restriction rather than extreme calorie restriction is sufficient to produce the observed health and longevity benefits in the studied rhesus monkeys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his book <i>How and Why We Age</i>, Hayflick says that caloric restriction may not be effective in humans, citing data from the Baltimore Longitudinal Study of Aging which shows that being thin does not favour longevity.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (February 2013)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayflick_1994_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hayflick_1994-153">[153]</a></sup> However, there may be confounders, e.g. smoking reduces both appetite and lifespan. Similarly, it is sometimes claimed that moderate <i>obesity</i> in later life may improve survival, but newer research has identified confounding factors such as weight loss due to terminal disease. Once these factors are accounted for, the optimal body weight above age 65 corresponds to a leaner <a href="/wiki/Body_mass_index" title="Body mass index">body mass index</a> of 23 to 27.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowman2016_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowman2016-154">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively, the benefits of dietary restriction can also be found by changing the <a href="/wiki/Nutrient" title="Nutrient">macro nutrient</a> profile to reduce protein intake without any changes to calorie level, resulting in similar increases in longevity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup> Dietary protein restriction not only inhibits mTOR activity but also IGF-1, two mechanisms implicated in ageing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fontana2010_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fontana2010-100">[100]</a></sup> Specifically, reducing <a href="/wiki/Leucine" title="Leucine">leucine</a> intake is sufficient to inhibit mTOR activity, achievable through reducing animal food consumption.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-158">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_diet" title="Mediterranean diet">Mediterranean diet</a> is credited with lowering the risk of heart disease and early death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18786971_160-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid18786971-160">[160]</a></sup> The major contributors to mortality risk reduction appear to be a higher consumption of vegetables, fish, fruits, nuts and monounsaturated fatty acids, i.e., olive oil.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deGaetano2016_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-deGaetano2016-161">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, the consumption of certain foods such as sugar, refined carbohydrates, alcohol, processed meats and fried food, accelerates ageing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>The amount of <a href="/wiki/Sleep" title="Sleep">sleep</a> has an impact on mortality. People who live the longest report sleeping for six to seven hours each night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> Lack of sleep (&lt;5 hours) more than doubles the risk of death from <a href="/wiki/Cardiovascular_disease" title="Cardiovascular disease">cardiovascular disease</a>, but too much sleep (&gt;9 hours) is associated with a doubling of the risk of death, though not primarily from cardiovascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> Sleeping more than 7 to 8 hours per day has been consistently associated with increased mortality, though the cause is probably other factors such as depression and socioeconomic status, which would correlate statistically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup> Sleep monitoring of hunter-gatherer tribes from Africa and from South America has shown similar sleep patterns across continents: their average sleeping duration is 6.4 hours (with a summer/winter difference of 1 hour), afternoon naps (<a href="/wiki/Siesta" title="Siesta">siestas</a>) are uncommon, and <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a> is very rare (tenfold less than in industrial societies).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_exercise" title="Physical exercise">Physical exercise</a> may increase life expectancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> People who participate in moderate to high levels of physical exercise have a lower mortality rate compared to individuals who are not physically active.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> Moderate levels of exercise have been correlated with preventing ageing and improving quality of life by reducing inflammatory potential.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> The majority of the benefits from exercise are achieved with around 3500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metabolic_equivalent" title="Metabolic equivalent">metabolic equivalent</a> (MET) minutes per week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2016_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2016-171">[171]</a></sup> For example, climbing stairs 10 minutes, vacuuming 15 minutes, gardening 20 minutes, running 20 minutes, and walking or bicycling for 25 minutes on a daily basis would <i>together</i> achieve about 3000 MET minutes a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BMJ2016_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-BMJ2016-171">[171]</a></sup> Other research seems to suggest a relationship between regular physical exercise and cognitive functioning in old age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>Avoidance of <a href="/wiki/Chronic_stress" title="Chronic stress">chronic stress</a> (as opposed to acute stress) is associated with a slower loss of <a href="/wiki/Telomere" title="Telomere">telomeres</a> in most but not all studies,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26318949_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26318949-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Quinlan2014_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Quinlan2014-174">[174]</a></sup> and with decreased <a href="/wiki/Cortisol" title="Cortisol">cortisol</a> levels. A chronically high cortisol level compromises the immune system, causes cardiac damage/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterosclerosis" title="Arterosclerosis">arterosclerosis</a> and is associated with facial ageing, and the latter in turn is a marker for increased morbidity and mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noordam2012_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noordam2012-175">[175]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lazzarino2013_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lazzarino2013-176">[176]</a></sup> A meta-analysis shows that loneliness carries a higher mortality risk than smoking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HoltLunstad2010_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-HoltLunstad2010-177">[177]</a></sup> Stress can be countered by social connection, spirituality, and (for men more clearly than for women) married life, all of which are associated with longevity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shor2012_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shor2012-181">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_intervention">Medical intervention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ageing&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Medical intervention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following drugs and interventions have been shown to slow or reverse the biological effects of ageing in animal models, but none has yet been proven to do so in humans.
